<p><b>Mount Athos</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/θ/ 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Άθως</span> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Greek" title="Help:IPA for Greek">[ˈaθos]</a></span>) is a mountain and <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a> in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and an important centre of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>. It is governed as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_autonomy" title="Territorial autonomy">autonomous polity</a> within the Greek Republic under the official name <b>Autonomous Monastic State of the Holy Mountain</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Αὐτόνομη Μοναστικὴ Πολιτεία Ἁγίου Ὄρους</span>). Mount Athos is home to 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monasteries" title="Monasteries">monasteries</a> under the direct jurisdiction of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a>.</p>
<p>Mount Athos is commonly referred to in Greek as the "<i>Holy Mountain</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Ἅγιον Ὄρος, <i>Agion Oros</i></span> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Greek" title="Help:IPA for Greek">[ˈaʝ(i)o(n) ˈoros]</a></span>) and the entity as the "<i>Athonite State</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Αθωνική Πολιτεία, <i>Athoniki Politia</i></span>). Other languages of orthodox tradition also use names translating to "Holy Mountain" (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> <span lang="bg" xml:lang="bg">Света гора</span> and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> მთაწმინდა). In the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical era</a>, while the mountain was called <i>Athos</i>, the peninsula was known as <i>Acté</i> or <i>Akté</i> (Ἀκτή).</p>
<p>Mount Athos has been inhabited since ancient times and is known for its nearly 1,800-year continuous Christian presence and its long historical monastic traditions, which date back to at least 800 A.D. and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine era</a>. Today, over 2,000 monks from Greece and many other Eastern Orthodox countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, live an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascetic" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> life in Athos, isolated from the rest of the world. The Athonite monasteries feature a rich collection of well-preserved artifacts, rare books, ancient documents, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Art" title="Byzantine Art">artworks</a> of immense historical value, and Mount Athos has been listed as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> since 1988.</p>
<p>Although Mount Athos is technically part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> like the rest of Greece, the status of the Monastic State of the Holy Mountain, and the jurisdiction of the Athonite institutions, were expressly described and ratified upon admission of Greece to the European Community (precursor to the EU). The free movement of people and goods in its territory is prohibited, unless formal permission is granted by the Monastic State's authorities, and only males are allowed to enter.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karta_Athos.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Karta_Athos.PNG/220px-Karta_Athos.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Karta_Athos.PNG/330px-Karta_Athos.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Karta_Athos.PNG/440px-Karta_Athos.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karta_Athos.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mont_Athos_3D_version_1.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="214" data-file-width="300" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Mont_Athos_3D_version_1.gif/280px-Mont_Athos_3D_version_1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bc/Mont_Athos_3D_version_1.gif 1.5x" width="280"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athos_peninsula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="700" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Athos_peninsula.jpg/220px-Athos_peninsula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Athos_peninsula.jpg/330px-Athos_peninsula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Athos_peninsula.jpg/440px-Athos_peninsula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athos_peninsula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The peninsula as seen from the summit of Mount Athos (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Mount_Athos&amp;params=40_9_28_N_24_19_36_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">40°9′28″N</span> <span class="longitude">24°19′36″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">40.15778°N 24.32667°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">40.15778; 24.32667</span></span></span></a></span>), looking north-west</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The peninsula, the easternmost "leg" of the larger <a href="/wiki/Chalkidiki" title="Chalkidiki">Chalkidiki</a> peninsula in central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Macedonia" title="Greek Macedonia">Macedonia</a>, protrudes 50 kilometres (31 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Draper-4">[4]</a></sup> into the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> at a width of between 7 and 12 kilometres (4.3 and 7.5 mi) and covers an area of 335.6 square kilometres (129.58 sq mi). The actual Mount Athos has steep, densely forested slopes reaching up to 2,033 metres (6,670 ft). The surrounding seas, especially at the end of the peninsula, can be dangerous. In ancient Greek history two fleet disasters in the area are recorded: In 492 BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius</a>, the king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, lost 300 ships under general <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a> (<a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> "Histories" book VI (Erato), <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a> "The Persians"). In 411 BC the <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a> lost a fleet of 50 ships under admiral <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Epicleas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Epicleas (page does not exist)">Epicleas</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>, "Bibliotheca historica" XIII 41, 1–3).</p>
<p>Though land-linked, Mount Athos is practically accessible only by ferry. The <i>Agios Panteleimon</i> and <i>Axion Estin</i> travel daily (weather permitting) between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ouranoupolis" title="Ouranoupolis">Ouranoupolis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dafni_(Athos)" title="Dafni (Athos)">Dafni</a>, with stops at some monasteries on the western coast. There is also a smaller speed boat, the <i>Agia Anna</i>, which travels the same route, but with no intermediate stops. It is possible to travel by ferry to and from <a href="/wiki/Ierissos" title="Ierissos">Ierissos</a> for direct access to monasteries along the eastern coast.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Access">Access</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Access">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The number of daily visitors to Mount Athos is restricted, and all are required to obtain a special entrance permit valid for a limited period. Only males are permitted to visit the territory, which is called the "Garden of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>" by the monks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> with Orthodox Christians taking precedence in permit issuance procedures. Residents on the peninsula must be males aged 18 and over who are members of the Eastern Orthodox Church and also either monks or workers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Athos_(mythology)" title="Athos (mythology)">Athos</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> is the name of one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigantes" title="Gigantes">Gigantes</a> that challenged the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_gods" title="Greek gods">Greek gods</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigantomachy" title="Gigantomachy">Gigantomachia</a>. Athos threw a massive rock against <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> which fell in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aegean_sea" title="Aegean sea">Aegean sea</a> and became Mount Athos. According to another version of the story, Poseidon used the mountain to bury the defeated giant.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> mentions the mountain Athos in the <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> writes that, during the Persian invasion of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> in 492 BC, the fleet of the Persian commander <a href="/wiki/Mardonius" title="Mardonius">Mardonius</a> was wrecked, with losses of 300 ships and 20,000 men, by a strong North wind while attempting to round the coast near Mount Athos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> mentions the peninsula, then called <i>Acte,</i> telling us that <a href="/wiki/Pelasgians" title="Pelasgians">Pelasgians</a> from the island of <a href="/wiki/Lemnos" title="Lemnos">Lemnos</a> populated it and naming five cities thereon, <a href="/wiki/Sane_(Acte)" title="Sane (Acte)">Sane</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleonae_(Chalcidice)" title="Cleonae (Chalcidice)">Cleonae</a> (Kleonai), Thyssos (Thyssus), Olophyxos (Olophyxis), and Acrothoï (Akrothoön).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> also mentions the cities of Dion (Dium) and Acrothoï.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eretria" title="Eretria">Eretria</a> also established colonies on Acte. At least one other city was established in the Classical period: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acanthus_(Greece)" title="Acanthus (Greece)">Acanthus</a> (Akanthos). Some of these cities minted their own coins.</p>
<p>The peninsula was on the invasion route of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, who spent three years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> excavating a <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_Canal" title="Xerxes Canal">channel</a> across the isthmus to allow the passage of his invasion fleet in 483 BC. After the death of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, the architect <a href="/wiki/Dinocrates" title="Dinocrates">Dinocrates</a> (Deinokrates) proposed to carve the entire mountain into a statue of Alexander.</p>
<p>The history of the peninsula during latter ages is shrouded by the lack of historical accounts. Archaeologists have not been able to determine the exact location of the cities reported by Strabo. It is believed that they must have been deserted when Athos' new inhabitants, the monks, started arriving some time before the ninth century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadas1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kadas1-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christianity">Early Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the Athonite tradition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Blessed Virgin Mary</a> was sailing accompanied by St <a href="/wiki/John_the_Evangelist" title="John the Evangelist">John the Evangelist</a> from <a href="/wiki/Jaffa" title="Jaffa">Joppa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> to visit <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_of_Bethany" title="Lazarus of Bethany">Lazarus</a>. When the ship was blown off course to then pagan Athos it was forced to anchor near the port of Klement, close to the present monastery of Iviron. The Virgin walked ashore and, overwhelmed by the wonderful and wild natural beauty of the mountain, she blessed it and asked her Son for it to be her garden. A voice was heard saying "<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἔστω ὁ τόπος οὖτος κλῆρος σὸς καὶ περιβόλαιον σὸν καὶ παράδεισος, ἔτι δὲ καὶ λιμὴν σωτήριος τῶν θελόντων σωθῆναι</span>" (Translation: "Let this place be your inheritance and your garden, a paradise and a haven of salvation for those seeking to be saved"). From that moment the mountain was consecrated as the garden of the Mother of God and was out of bounds to all other women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[note 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historical documents on ancient Mount Athos history are very few. It is certain that monks have been there since the fourth century, and possibly since the third. During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a>'s reign (324–337) both Christians and pagans were living there. During the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian the Apostate</a> (361–363), the churches of Mount Athos were destroyed, and Christians hid in the woods and inaccessible places.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Julian was not known to destroy any Christian churches, but simply restore pagan temples that had been confiscated and reconsecrated as churches, per his edict of religious toleration. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Later, during <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>'s reign (379–395), the pagan temples were destroyed. The lexicographer <a href="/wiki/Hesychius_of_Alexandria" title="Hesychius of Alexandria">Hesychius of Alexandria</a> states that in the fifth century there was still a temple and a statue of "<a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> Athonite". After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Islamic conquest of Egypt">Islamic conquest of Egypt</a> in the seventh century, many orthodox monks from the Egyptian desert tried to find another calm place; some of them came to the Athos peninsula. An ancient document states that monks "built huts of wood with roofs of straw [...] and by collecting fruit from the wild trees were providing themselves improvised meals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_era:_the_first_monasteries">Byzantine era: the first monasteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Byzantine era: the first monasteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="232" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg/150px-Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg/225px-Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nikiphoros_Phokas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_II_Phokas" title="Nikephoros II Phokas">Nicephorus Phocas</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athos2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1110" data-file-width="1620" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Athos2.jpg/200px-Athos2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Athos2.jpg/300px-Athos2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Athos2.jpg/400px-Athos2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athos2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Byzantine watch tower, protecting the "arsanas" (αρσανάς, =dock) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiropotamou_monastery" title="Xiropotamou monastery">Xiropotamou monastery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athanasios.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="290" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Athanasios.jpg/180px-Athanasios.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Athanasios.jpg/270px-Athanasios.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Athanasios.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athanasios.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasios_the_Athonite" title="Athanasios the Athonite">Athanasios the Athonite</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The chroniclers <a href="/wiki/Theophanes_the_Confessor" title="Theophanes the Confessor">Theophanes the Confessor</a> (end of eighth century) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgios_Kedrenos" title="Georgios Kedrenos">Georgios Kedrenos</a> (11th century) wrote that the 726 eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Santorini" title="Santorini">Thera volcano</a> was visible from Mount Athos, indicating that it was inhabited at the time. The historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genesios" title="Genesios">Genesios</a> recorded that monks from Athos participated at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">seventh Ecumenical Council of Nicaea</a> of 787. Following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thasos" title="Battle of Thasos">Battle of Thasos</a> in 829, Athos was deserted for some time due to the destructive raids of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretan_Saracens" title="Cretan Saracens">Cretan Saracens</a>. Around 860, the famous monk Efthymios the Younger came to Athos and a number of monk-huts ("skete of Saint Basil") were created around his habitation, possibly near Krya Nera. During the reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> the Macedonian, the former Archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> (and later of <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>) Basil the Confessor built a small monastery at the place of the modern harbour ("arsanas") of Hilandariou Monastery. Soon after this, a document of 883 states that a certain Ioannis Kolovos built a monastery at Megali Vigla.</p>
<p>On a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysobull" title="Chrysobull">chrysobull</a> of emperor Basil I, dated 885, the Holy Mountain is proclaimed a place of monks, and no laymen or farmers or cattle-breeders are allowed to be settled there. The next year, in an imperial edict of emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a> we read about the "so-called ancient seat of the council of <a href="/wiki/Starets" title="Starets">gerondes</a> (council of elders)", meaning that there was already a kind of monks' administration and that it was already "ancient". In 887, some monks expostulate to the emperor Leo the Wise as the monastery of Kolovos is growing more and more and they lose their peace.</p>
<p>In 908, the existence of a <i><a href="/wiki/Protos_(monastic_office)" title="Protos (monastic office)">Protos</a></i> ("First monk"), the "head" of the monastic community, is documented. In 943, the borders of the monastic state were precisely mapped while we know that Karyes is already the capital town and seat of the administration and has the name "Megali Mesi Lavra" (Big Central Assembly). In 956, a decree offered land of about 940,000 m<sup>2</sup> (10,118,075.79 sq ft) to the Xiropotamou monastery, which means that this monastery was already quite big.</p>
<p>In 958, the monk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasios_the_Athonite" title="Athanasios the Athonite">Athanasios the Athonite</a> (Άγιος Αθανάσιος ο Αθωνίτης) arrived on Mount Athos. In 962, he built the big central church of the "Protaton" in Karies. In the next year, with the support of his friend, Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikephoros_II" title="Nikephoros II">Nicephorus Phocas</a>, the monastery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lavra_(Athos)" title="Great Lavra (Athos)">Great Lavra</a> was founded, still the largest and most prominent of the twenty monasteries existing today. It enjoyed the protection of the emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> during the following centuries, and its wealth and possessions grew considerably. The <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> in the 13th century brought new Roman Catholic overlords which forced the monks to complain and ask for the intervention of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a>, until the restoration of the Byzantine Empire came. The peninsula was raided by <a href="/wiki/Catalan_Company" title="Catalan Company">Catalan mercenaries</a> in the 14th century, a century that also saw the theological conflict over the <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychasm</a> practised on Mount Athos and defended by <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Palamas" title="Gregory Palamas">Gregory Palamas</a> (Άγιος Γρηγόριος ο Παλαμάς). In late 1371 or early 1372 the Byzantines defeated an Ottoman attack on Athos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_era">Ottoman era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ottoman era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Byzantine Empire was conquered in the 15th century and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> took its place. The Athonite monks tried to maintain good relations with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Sultan" title="Ottoman Sultan">Ottoman Sultans</a> and therefore when <a href="/wiki/Murad_II" title="Murad II">Murad II</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a> in 1430 they immediately pledged allegiance to him. In return, Murad recognized the monasteries' properties, something which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" title="Mehmed II">Mehmed II</a> formally ratified after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453. In this way the Athonite independence was somewhat guaranteed.</p>
<p>From the account of Russian pilgrim Isaiah by the end of the 15th century half of the monasteries were either Slav or Albanian, in particular, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Docheiariou_monastery" title="Docheiariou monastery">Docheiariou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osiou_Gregoriou_monastery" title="Osiou Gregoriou monastery">Grigoriou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agiou_Pavlou_monastery" title="Agiou Pavlou monastery">Ayiou Pavlou</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dionysiou_monastery" title="Dionysiou monastery">Ayiou Dionysiou</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hilandar" title="Hilandar">Chilandariou</a> were Serbian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karakalou_monastery" title="Karakalou monastery">Karakalou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philotheou_monastery" title="Philotheou monastery">Philotheou</a> were Albanian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agiou_Panteleimonos_monastery" title="Agiou Panteleimonos monastery">Panteleïmon</a> was Russian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simonopetra_monastery" title="Simonopetra monastery">Simonopetra</a> was Bulgarian, Pantokratoros and Stavronikita was Greek while he mentions that Zographou, Kastamonitou, Xeropotamou, Koutloumousiou, Xenophontos, Iviron and Protaton did not bear any designation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakalopulos1973_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bakalopulos1973-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 15th and 16th centuries were particularly peaceful for the Athonite community. This led to relative prosperity for the monasteries. An example of this is the foundation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stavronikita_monastery" title="Stavronikita monastery">Stavronikita monastery</a> which completed the current number of Athonite monasteries. Following the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Despotate" title="Serbian Despotate">Serbian Despotate</a> by the Ottomans many Serbian monks came to Athos. The extensive presence of Serbian monks is depicted in the numerous elections of Serbian monks to the office of the <a href="/wiki/Protos_(monastic_office)" title="Protos (monastic office)">Protos</a> during the era.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vatopedi_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Vatopedi_2.jpg/220px-Vatopedi_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Vatopedi_2.jpg/330px-Vatopedi_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Vatopedi_2.jpg/440px-Vatopedi_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vatopedi_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of the area around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vatopedi_monastery" title="Vatopedi monastery">Vatopedi monastery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Sultan Selim I</a> was a substantial benefactor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiropotamou_monastery" title="Xiropotamou monastery">Xiropotamou monastery</a>. In 1517, he issued a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatw%C4%81" title="Fatwā">fatwa</a> and a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hatt-i_Sharif" title="Hatt-i Sharif">Hatt-i Sharif</a></i> ("noble edict") that "the place, where the Holy Gospel is preached, whenever it is burned or even damaged, shall be erected again." He also endowed privileges to the Abbey and financed the construction of the dining area and underground of the Abbey as well as the renovation of the wall paintings in the central church that were completed between the years 1533–1541.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although most time the monasteries were left on their own, the Ottomans heavily taxed them and sometimes they seized important land parcels from them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This eventually culminated in an economic crisis in Athos during the 17th century. This led to the adoption of the so-called "idiorrhythmic" lifestyle (a <a href="/wiki/Hermit#Semi-eremitic_variants" title="Hermit">semi-eremitic variant</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">Christian monasticism</a>) by a few monasteries at first and later, during the first half of the 18th century, by all.</p>
<p>This new way of monastic organization was an emergency measure taken by the monastic communities to counter their harsh economic environment. Contrary to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cenobitic" title="Cenobitic">cenobitic</a> system, monks in idiorrhythmic communities have private property, work for themselves, they are solely responsible for acquiring food and other necessities and they dine separately in their cells, only meeting with other monks at church. At the same time, the monasteries' <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbots</a> were replaced by committees and at Karyes the Protos was replaced by a four-member committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skadas2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skadas2-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1749, with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Athonite_Academy" title="Athonite Academy">Athonite Academy</a> near Vatopedi monastery, the local <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monastic</a> community took a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_Enlightenment" title="Modern Greek Enlightenment">modern Greek Enlightenment</a> movement of the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> This institution offered high level education, especially under <a href="/wiki/Eugenios_Voulgaris" title="Eugenios Voulgaris">Eugenios Voulgaris</a>, where ancient philosophy and modern physical science were taught.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> tsars, and princes from <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> (until the end of the 15th century), helped the monasteries survive with large donations. The population of monks and their wealth declined over the next centuries, but were revitalized during the 19th century, particularly by the patronage of the Russian government. As a result, the monastic population grew steadily throughout the century, reaching a high point of over 7,000 monks in 1902.</p>
<p>In November 1912, during the <a href="/wiki/First_Balkan_War" title="First Balkan War">First Balkan War</a>, the Ottomans were forced out by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Navy" title="Greek Navy">Greek Navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Greece claimed the peninsula as part of the peace <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1913)" title="Treaty of London (1913)">treaty of London</a> signed on 30 May 1913. As a result of the shortcomings of the Treaty of London, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Balkan_War" title="Second Balkan War">Second Balkan War</a> broke out between the combatants in June 1913. A final peace was agreed at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Bucharest_(1913)" title="Treaty of Bucharest (1913)">Treaty of Bucharest</a> on 10 August 1913.</p>
<p>In June 1913, a small Russian fleet, consisting of the gunboat <i>Donets</i> and the transport ships <i>Tsar</i> and <i>Kherson</i>, delivered the archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Vologda" title="Vologda">Vologda</a>, and a number of troops to Mount Athos to intervene in the theological controversy over <i><a href="/wiki/Imiaslavie" title="Imiaslavie">imiaslavie</a></i> (a Russian Orthodox movement).</p>
<p>The archbishop held talks with the <i>imiaslavtsy</i> and tried to make them change their beliefs voluntarily, but was unsuccessful. On 31 July 1913, the troops stormed the St. Panteleimon Monastery. Although the monks were not armed and did not actively resist, the troops showed very heavy-handed tactics. After the storming of St. Panteleimon Monastery, the monks from the Andreevsky Skete (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skiti_Agiou_Andrea" title="Skiti Agiou Andrea">Skiti Agiou Andrea</a>) surrendered voluntarily. The military transport <i>Kherson</i> was converted into a prison ship and more than a thousand <i>imiaslavtsy</i> monks were sent to Odessa where they were excommunicated and dispersed throughout Russia.</p>
<p>After a brief diplomatic conflict between Greece and Russia over sovereignty, the peninsula formally came under Greek sovereignty after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:658px;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg/200px-Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg/300px-Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg/400px-Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Athos_by_cod_gabriel_36.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agiou_Panteleimonos_monastery" title="Agiou Panteleimonos monastery">Agiou Panteleimonos monastery</a>, traditional home of <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian</a> monks, was the main theater of the <a href="/wiki/Imiaslavie" title="Imiaslavie">Imiaslavie</a> dogmatic controversy during the early 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The self-governed region of the Holy Mountain, according to the Decree passed by the Holy Community on 3 October 1913 and according to the international treaties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London,_1913" title="Treaty of London, 1913">London</a> (1913), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Bucharest,_1913" title="Treaty of Bucharest, 1913">Bucharest</a> (1913), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Neuilly" title="Treaty of Neuilly">Neuilly</a> (1919), <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of Sèvres">Sèvres</a> (1920) and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Lausanne</a> (1923), is considered part of the Greek state. The Decree, <i>"made in the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Axion_Estin#Icon_and_legend" title="Axion Estin">Holy Icon of Axion Estin</a>"</i>, stated that the Holy Community recognised the Kings of Greece as the lawful sovereigns and "successors on the Mountain" of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">"Emperors who built"</a> the monasteries and declared its territory as belonging to the then Kingdom of Greece.</p>
<p>Political instability in Greece during the mid-20th century that affected Mount Athos included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_occupation_of_Greece_during_World_War_II" title="Axis occupation of Greece during World War II">Nazi occupation</a> from the Easter season of 1941 through late 1944, followed immediately by the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a> in a struggle where Communist efforts failed. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Greece" title="Battle of Greece">Battle of Greece</a> was reported in <i>Time</i> magazine, "The Stukas swooped across the Aegean skies like dark, dreadful birds, but they dropped no bombs on the monks of Mount Athos".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIME_Apr.2741_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIME_Apr.2741-21">[20]</a></sup> After the Nazi takeover of Greece, the Epistassia, Athos's four-member executive committee, formally asked Hitler to place the Autonomous Monastic State under his personal protection, and Hitler agreed. Mount Athos survived World War II nearly untouched, and for the remainder of the war, the monks of Mount Athos referred to Adolf Hitler as "High Protector of the Holy Mountain" (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Hoher Protektor des heiligen Berges</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HitlerEra_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-HitlerEra-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later a "Special Double Assembly" of the Holy Community in Karyes passed the "Constitutional Charter" of the Holy Mountain, which was ratified by the Greek Parliament. This regime originates from the <i>"self-ruled monastic state"</i> as stated on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysobull" title="Chrysobull">chrysobull</a> parchment signed and sealed by the Byzantine Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Tzimisces" title="John Tzimisces">Ioannis Tzimisces</a> in 972.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This important document is preserved in the House of the Holy Administration in Karyes. The self-rule of the Holy Mountain was later reaffirmed by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I Komnenos</a> in 1095.</p>
<p>According to the constitution of Greece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekConstitution_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreekConstitution-23">[22]</a></sup> Mount Athos (the "Monastic State of Agion Oros") is, <i>"following ancient privilege"</i>, "a self-governed part of the Greek State, whose sovereignty thereon shall remain intact", and consists of 20 main monasteries which constitute the Holy Community, and the capital town and administrative centre, Karyes, also home to a governor as the representative of the Greek state. The governor is an executive appointee. The status of the Holy Mountain and the jurisdiction of the Agiorite institutions were expressly described and ratified upon admission of Greece to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (then the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Community" title="European Community">European Community</a>).</p>
<p>On 11 September 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Alexandria" title="Patriarch of Alexandria">Eastern Orthodox Patriarch of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Peter_VII_of_Alexandria" title="Patriarch Peter VII of Alexandria">Peter VII</a>, was killed, together with 16 others, when a Greek military Chinook helicopter in which he was travelling crashed in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> off the peninsula. The Patriarch was heading to Mount Athos. The cause of the crash remains unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monasteries of Mount Athos have a history of opposing <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenism</a>, or movements towards reconciliation between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_of_Constantinople" title="Orthodox Church of Constantinople">Orthodox Church of Constantinople</a> and the Roman Catholic Church. The <a href="/wiki/Esphigmenou" title="Esphigmenou">Esphigmenou</a> monastery is particularly outspoken in this respect, having raised black flags to protest against the meeting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Athenagoras_I_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Athenagoras I of Constantinople">Patriarch Athenagoras I of Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_VI" title="Pope Paul VI">Pope Paul VI</a> in 1972. Esphigmenou was subsequently expelled from the representative bodies of the Athonite Community. The conflict escalated in 2002 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Bartholomew I of Constantinople">Patriarch Bartholomew I of Constantinople</a> declaring the monks of Esphigmenou an illegal brotherhood and ordering their eviction; the monks refused to be evicted, and the Patriarch ordered a new brotherhood to replace them.</p>
<p>After reaching a low point of just 1,145 mainly elderly monks in 1971, the monasteries have been undergoing a steady and sustained renewal. By the year 2000, the monastic population had reached 1,610, with all 20 monasteries and their associated sketes receiving an infusion of mainly young well-educated monks. In 2009, the population stood at nearly 2,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draper_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Draper-4">[4]</a></sup> Many younger monks possess university education and advanced skills that allow them to work on the cataloging and restoration of the Mountain's vast repository of manuscripts, vestments, icons, liturgical objects and other works of art, most of which remain unknown to the public because of their sheer volume. Projected to take several decades to complete, this restorative and archival work is well under way, funded by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> and the EU, and aided by many academic institutions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_organization">Administration and organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Administration and organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/150px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/225px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/300px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The flag used by the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>, which is also the flag of Mount Athos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRC_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRC-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitaliev1995_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitaliev1995-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crampton1990_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crampton1990-28">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Athos is governed by the "Holy Community" (Ιερά Κοινότητα – Iera Koinotita) which consists of the representatives of the 20 Holy Monasteries, having as executive committee the four-membered "Holy Administration" (Ιερά Επιστασία – Iera Epistasia), with the <a href="/wiki/Protos_(monastic_office)" title="Protos (monastic office)">Protos</a> (Πρώτος) being its head.</p>
<p>Civil authorities are represented by the Civil Governor, appointed by the Greek Ministry of Foreign Affairs, whose main duty is to supervise the function of the institutions and the public order. The current Civil Governor is <a href="/wiki/Aristos_Kasmiroglou" title="Aristos Kasmiroglou">Aristos Kasmiroglou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In each of the 20 monasteries – which today all follow again the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenobitic" title="Coenobitic">coenobitic</a> system – the administration is in the hands of the Abbot (Ηγούμενος – Hēgoumenos) who is elected by the brotherhood for life. He is the lord and spiritual father of the monastery. The Convention of the brotherhood (Γεροντία) is the legislative body. All the other establishments (sketes, cells, huts, retreats, hermitages) are dependencies of some of the 20 monasteries and are assigned to the monks by a document called "<i>homologon</i>" (ομόλογον).</p>
<p>All persons leading a monastic life thereon acquire Greek citizenship without further formalities, upon admission as novices or monks. Visits to the peninsula are possible for laymen, but they need a special permit known as a <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ISO 843 Greek"><i>diamonētērion</i></span> (<span lang="el" xml:lang="el">διαμονητήριον</span>), similar to a visa.</p>
<p>Of the 20 monasteries located on the Holy Mountain, the brethren of 17 are predominantly ethnically Greek. Of the other 3, brethren are drawn from monks of primarily other origins, who become Greek subjects. These are the <a href="/wiki/Hilandar" title="Hilandar">Helandariou Monastery</a> (Serbian), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zograf_Monastery" title="Zograf Monastery">Zografou Monastery</a> (Bulgarian) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agiou_Panteleimonos_monastery" title="Agiou Panteleimonos monastery">Agiou Panteleimonos Monastery</a> (Russian).</p>
<p>Among the sketes, most are predominantly ethnic Greek. However, two are Romanian, the coenobitic "<a href="/wiki/Prodromos_(Mount_Athos)" title="Prodromos (Mount Athos)">Skētē Timiou Prodromou</a>" (which belongs to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lavra_(Athos)" title="Great Lavra (Athos)">Megistis Lavras Monastery</a>) and the idiorrythmic "Skētē Agiou Dēmētriou tou Lakkou", also called "<a href="/wiki/Lakkoskiti" title="Lakkoskiti">Lakkoskētē</a>" (which belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Agiou_Pavlou_monastery" title="Agiou Pavlou monastery">Agiou Pavlou Monastery</a>). Another one is Bulgarian, "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Skiti_Vogoroditsa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Skiti Vogoroditsa (page does not exist)">Skētē Bogoroditsa</a>" (which belongs to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agiou_Panteleimonos_monastery" title="Agiou Panteleimonos monastery">Agiou Panteleimonos Monastery</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visiting_procedure">Visiting procedure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Visiting procedure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diamonitirion_1978.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3382" data-file-width="2475" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diamonitirion_1978.jpg/180px-Diamonitirion_1978.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diamonitirion_1978.jpg/270px-Diamonitirion_1978.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diamonitirion_1978.jpg/360px-Diamonitirion_1978.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diamonitirion_1978.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diamonētērion of 1978</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Entry to the mountain is usually by ferry boat either from the port of Ouranoupoli (for west coast monasteries) or from Ierrisos for those on the east coast. Before embarking on the boat all visitors must have been issued a <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ISO 843 Greek">diamonētērion</span> (<span lang="el" xml:lang="el">διαμονητήριον</span>), a form of Byzantine visa that is written in Greek, dated using the Julian calendar, and signed by four of the secretaries of leading monasteries. Clergy of the Orthodox Church are required to obtain permission of the Patriarch of Constantinople. For laymen there are generally two kinds of diamonētēria: the general diamonētērion that enables the visitor to stay overnight at any one of the monasteries but only to stay in the mountain for several days (more in winter, when there are fewer visitors, than in summer), and the special diamonētērion which allows a visitor to visit only one monastery or <a href="/wiki/Skete" title="Skete">skete</a> but to stay as many days as he has agreed with the monks. Laymen are required to have short hair and any non-cleric arriving on Athos with long hair has his hair cut. The general diamonētērion is available upon application to the Pilgrims' Bureau in Thessaloniki. Once this has been granted it will be issued at the port of departure, on the day of departure. Once granted, the pilgrim can contact the monastery where he would like to stay in order to reserve a bed (one night only per monastery). The ferries require reservations, both ways.</p>
<p>The duration of the general visa can be extended by several days by personally applying at the main office in Karyes or, as is done typically for someone seeking to become a monk, by a request from a monastery.</p>
<p>Most visitors arrive at the small port of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dafni_(Athos)" title="Dafni (Athos)">Dafni</a> from where they can take the only paved road in the mountain to the capital Karyes or continue via another smaller boat to other monasteries down the coast. There is a public bus between Dafni and Karyes. Expensive taxis operated by monks are available for hire at Dafni and Karyes. They are all-wheel drive vehicles since most roads in the mountain are unpaved. Visitors to monasteries on the mountain's western side prefer to stay on the ferry and disembark at the monastery they wish to visit.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibition_on_entry_for_women">Prohibition on entry for women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Prohibition on entry for women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg/300px-Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg/450px-Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg/600px-Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athos_grenze_frangokastro_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sign at entrance to Mount Athos</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is a prohibition on entry for women, called <i>avaton</i> (Άβατον) in Greek, to make living in celibacy easier for men who have chosen to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifpa_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifpa-30">[29]</a></sup> Monks feel that the presence of women alters the social dynamics of the community and therefore slows their path towards spiritual enlightenment. The ban was officially proclaimed by several emperors, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_Monomachos" title="Constantine Monomachos">Constantine Monomachos</a>, in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysobull" title="Chrysobull">chrysobull</a> of 1046.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 14th century, Serbian Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stephen_Uro%C5%A1_IV_Du%C5%A1an_of_Serbia" title="Stephen Uroš IV Dušan of Serbia">Dušan the Mighty</a> brought his wife, <a href="/wiki/Helena_of_Bulgaria" title="Helena of Bulgaria">Helena of Bulgaria</a>, to Mount Athos to protect her from the plague, but she did not touch the ground during her entire visit, as she was carried in a <a href="/wiki/Carriage" title="Carriage">hand carriage</a> all the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>French writer <a href="/wiki/Maryse_Choisy" title="Maryse Choisy">Maryse Choisy</a> entered Mount Athos in the 1920s disguised as a sailor, and later wrote about her escapade in <i>Un mois chez les hommes</i> ("A Month With Men").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was an incident in the 1930s regarding <a href="/wiki/Aliki_Diplarakou" title="Aliki Diplarakou">Aliki Diplarakou</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> beauty pageant contestant to win the <a href="/wiki/Miss_Europe" title="Miss Europe">Miss Europe</a> title, who shocked the world when she dressed up as a man and sneaked into Mount Athos. Her escapade was discussed in the 13 July 1953, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine article entitled "The Climax of Sin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time1953_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time1953-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1953, Cora Miller, an American <a href="/wiki/Fulbright_Program" title="Fulbright Program">Fulbright Program</a> teacher from <a href="/wiki/Athens,_Ohio" title="Athens, Ohio">Athens, Ohio</a>, landed briefly along with two other women, stirring up a controversy among the local monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2003 resolution of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> requested the lifting of the ban for violating "the universally recognised principle of gender equality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 26 May 2008, five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moldovan_people" title="Moldovan people">Moldovans</a> illegally entered Greece by way of Turkey, ending up on Athos; four of the migrants were women. The monks forgave them for trespassing and informed them that the area was forbidden to females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Female animals, <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cow" title="Cow">cows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">ewes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">nanny-goats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mares</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">sows</a> are also barred except for female <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a>, female <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and female <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">songbirds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_in_the_European_Union">Status in the European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Status in the European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of an EU member state, Mount Athos is part of the European Union and, for the most part, subject to EU law. While outside the EU's Value Added Tax area, Mount Athos is part of the Schengen Area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> A declaration attached to Greece's accession treaty to the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Agreement" title="Schengen Agreement">Schengen Agreement</a> states that Mount Athos' "special status" should be taken into account in the application of the Schengen rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The monks strongly objected to Greece joining the Schengen Area based on fears that the EU would be able to end the centuries-old prohibition on the admittance of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1998_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1998-42">[41]</a></sup> However the prohibition persists and a special permit is required to enter the peninsula. The monks are also worried that the agreement could affect their traditional right to offer sanctuary to people from orthodox countries such as Russia and Serbia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1998_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1998-42">[41]</a></sup> Such monks do nowadays need a Greek visa and permission to stay, even if that is given generously by the Greek ministry, based on requests from Athos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_life_in_the_Agion_Oros">Culture and life in the Agion Oros</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Culture and life in the Agion Oros">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_literary_treasures">Art and literary treasures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Art and literary treasures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="678" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg/200px-Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg/300px-Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg/400px-Jesus_in_Golgotha_by_Theophanes_the_Cretan.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vatopedion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="748" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vatopedion.jpg/200px-Vatopedion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vatopedion.jpg/300px-Vatopedion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Vatopedion.jpg/400px-Vatopedion.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>

Decorated wall of the Catholicon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vatopedi_monastery" title="Vatopedi monastery">Vatopedi monastery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1848" data-file-width="2706" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG/200px-Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG/300px-Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG/400px-Saint_Mercurius_and_Artemius_of_Antioch.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pantokrator_13cent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="973" data-file-width="737" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pantokrator_13cent.jpg/200px-Pantokrator_13cent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pantokrator_13cent.jpg/300px-Pantokrator_13cent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pantokrator_13cent.jpg/400px-Pantokrator_13cent.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pantokrator_13cent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An icon of Jesus, <a href="/wiki/Hilandar" title="Hilandar">Hilandar monastery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Athonite monasteries possess huge deposits of invaluable <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> art treasures, including <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icons</a>, liturgical <a href="/wiki/Vestment" title="Vestment">vestments</a> and objects (crosses, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalice_(cup)" title="Chalice (cup)">chalices</a>), <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codices</a> and other Christian texts, imperial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysobull" title="Chrysobull">chrysobulls</a>, holy <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> etc. However the monks consider them for their religious function only, not as "treasures" and most are in regular use for their original purpose. Until recently no organized study and archiving had been carried out, but an EU-funded effort to catalogue, protect and restore them is underway since the late 1980s. Their sheer number is such, it is estimated that several decades will pass before the work is completed.</p>
<p>Among the most ancient and priceless codices at Mount Athos are the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Athous_Lavrensis" title="Codex Athous Lavrensis">Codex Athous Lavrensis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Athous_Dionysiou" title="Codex Athous Dionysiou">Codex Athous Dionysiou</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> is commonly used in all the Greek monasteries, but in some monasteries there are other languages in use: in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Pantaleon_Monastery" title="St. Pantaleon Monastery">Agiou Panteleimonos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> (67 monks in 2011); in <a href="/wiki/Hilandar" title="Hilandar">Helandariou Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> (58); in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zographou_Monastery" title="Zographou Monastery">Zographou Monastery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skiti_Bogoroditsa" title="Skiti Bogoroditsa">Skiti Bogoroditsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> (32); and in the sketes of <a href="/wiki/Prodromos_(Mount_Athos)" title="Prodromos (Mount Athos)">Timiou Prodromou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lakkoskiti" title="Lakkoskiti">Lakkoskiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> (64). Today, many of the Greek monks also speak foreign languages. Since there are monks from many nations in Athos, they naturally also speak their own native languages.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_time_reckoning">Date and time reckoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Date and time reckoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>, which currently has a difference of 13 days from the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>, is still used on Mount Athos. In 1923, as a means to eliminate the divergence existing between the religious and civil dates, after a <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, part of the Eastern Orthodox Churches dropped 13 days and adopted the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Julian_calendar" title="Revised Julian calendar">Revised Julian calendar</a>, which is synchronised with the Gregorian calendar, at least until 2800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[note 2]</a></sup> However, although under the direct jurisdiction of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>, the spiritual head of the monastic state, nearly all the monasteries of Athos refused to follow the revised calendar and finally, for the sake of uniformity, the patriarch asked the single monastery that used the revised calendar to revert to the Julian.</p>
<p>Also in use is <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_time" title="Byzantine time">Byzantine time</a>, in which the day commences at sunset as does the liturgical day<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[note 3]</a></sup> and not at midnight as in the reckoning of civil time, and the difference between the two varies according to the season of the year. Because the time interval from sundown to sundown is not constant, clocks showing the Byzantine time require continual manual readjusting which in current practice is done weekly, on Saturday, if the sky is clear; where the summit of Athos is visible, 12:00 is set when the last rays of sunlight cease to shine on the tip. Some monasteries also have a clock showing civil time since boat schedules run thereon (and on the civil calendar) as well as for pilgrims who may be disoriented by Byzantine time reckoning.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monastic_life:_monasteries.2C_sketae.2C_and_cells">Monastic life: monasteries, sketae, and cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Monastic life: monasteries, sketae, and cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As described above, today the 20 monasteries of Mount Athos are the dominant holy institutions for both spiritual and administrative purposes, consolidated by the Constitutional Chart of the Holy Mountain. Although, since the beginning of Mount Athos' history, monks were living in lodgings of different size and construction quality. All these monastic lodging types exist until today, named as seats (καθίσματα), cells (κελλιά), huts (καλύβες), retreats (ησυχαστήρια), hermitages (ερημιτήρια), caves (σπήλαια), sketai (σκήτες) and all of them are known under the general term "dependencies" (εξαρτήματα) of the Holy Monasteries. The term "cells" can be used under a more generalised meaning, comprising all the above but sketae, and following this term we can talk about three different kind of institutions in Mount Athos: monasteries, sketae and cells.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monasteries">Monasteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Monasteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some information is already given above, in the section "Administration and organization". A pilgrim/visitor to a monastery, who is accommodated in the guest-house (αρχονταρίκι) can have a taste of the monastic life in it by following its daily schedule: praying (services in church or in private), common dining, working (according to the duties of each monk) and rest. During religious celebrations usually long vigils are held and the entire daily program is radically reshaped. The gate of the monastery closes by sunset and opens again by sunrise.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A cell is a house with a small church, where 1–3 monks live under the spiritual and administrative supervision of a monastery. Monastic life in the cells is totally different from that in a monastery. Some of the cells resemble tidy farmhouses, others are poor huts, others have the gentility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> tradition or of Russian architecture of the past century. Usually, each cell possesses a piece of land for agricultural or other use. Each cell has to organize some activities for income. Besides the traditional occupations (agriculture, fishing, woodcarving, spirit distillation, iconography, tailoring, book binding etc.) new occupations have been taken up, for example taxi driving, couriers, car repairing and computer services.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The monk(s) living in a cell, having to take care of all daily chores, make up their own schedules. For the pilgrim/visitor it is worth experiencing this side of monastic life as well, but most of the cells have very limited or no capacity for hospitality.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sketes">Sketes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sketes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Skete" title="Skete">Skete</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg/240px-Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg/360px-Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg/480px-Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Athos-_Nea_Skiti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <i>Nea Skiti</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Small communities of neighbouring cells were developed since the beginning of monastic life on Mount Athos and some of them were using the word "skete" (σκήτη) meaning "monastic settlement" or "lavra" (λαύρα) meaning "monastic congregation". The word "skete" is of Coptic origin and in its original form is a placename of a location in the Egyptian desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> It is in the Egyptian desert where monasticism made its first steps. The unknown author of the "History of the Egyptian Monks" (Historia Monachorum in Aegypto), perhaps <a href="/wiki/Rufinus_(consul)" title="Rufinus (consul)">Flavius Rufinus</a> visited the area at the end of the fourth century. He tells us: "Then we came to <a href="/wiki/Nitria_(monastic_site)" title="Nitria (monastic site)">Nitria</a>, the best-known of all monasteries of Egypt, about forty miles [60 km] from Alexandria; it takes its name from a nearby town where Nitre is collected... In this place there are about fifty dwellings, or not many less, set near together and under one father. In some of them, there are many living together, in others a few and in some there are brothers who live alone. Though they are divided by their dwellings they remain bound together and inseparable in faith and love". This is exactly the main idea of a "skete", the communal way, just between the eremetic way and the coenobitic way of monasticism, with all 3 coexisting until today.</p>
<p>In 1680 the ex-patriarch <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dionysios_III_Vardalis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dionysios III Vardalis (page does not exist)">Dionysios III Vardalis</a> built in the Saint Anne skete of the Holy Mountain a big central church to accommodate all the monks of the area and in 1689 an internal regulatory text was constituted by the monks and ratified first by the Monastery of Megisti Lavra and finally by the patriarch <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dionysios_V_Haritonidis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dionysios V Haritonidis (page does not exist)">Dionysios V Haritonidis</a>; and later again by patriarch <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kyrilos_V&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kyrilos V (page does not exist)">Kyrilos V</a>, who contributed in its evolution. Since then, more sketes followed on the same way, and gradually the term "skete" (within the Holy Mountain) came to be used only for the monastic settlements having an internal rule ratified by the Patriarchate.</p>
<p>Later on, some cells came to attract many monks, expanded their buildings and started functioning in the coenobitic way of the monasteries. Since the number of the Monasteries in Mount Athos was restricted to 20, a new term was introduced: the <b>"coenobitic skete"</b> (κοινόβιος σκήτη), while a skete of the traditional form was named <b>"idiorrythmic skete"</b> (ιδιόρρυθμος σκήτη) in order to underline the difference.</p>
<p>The first ones, both in architecture and life-style, follow the typical model of a monastery, that of a community living together, sharing and distributing work, and praying together daily. In contrast, the idiorrhythmic community (intermediary between the ceonobitic community and the seclusion of a <a href="/wiki/Hermit" title="Hermit">hermit</a>) resembles a hamlet, and the daily life there is much like that of a cell. But there are also some duties for the community. Near the centre of the settlement is the central church called <i>Kyriakon</i> (Κυριακόν, that could be translated "for Sunday"), where the whole brotherhood meets for the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> service, on Sundays and on greater feasts. Usually there are also an administration house, a refectory for common celebrations, a cemetery, a library, storehouses and a guesthouse.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_religious_institutions">List of religious institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: List of religious institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Twelve_sketes">Twelve sketes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Athos&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Twelve sketes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Skete" title="Skete">skete</a> is a community of Christian hermits following a monastic rule, allowing them to worship in comparative solitude, while also affording them a level of mutual practical support and security. There are two kinds of sketes in Mount Athos. A koenobitic skete follows the style of monasteries. An idiorrhythmic skete follows the style of a small village: it has a common area of worship (a church), with individual hermitages or small houses around it, each one for a small number of occupants. There are 12 official sketes on Mount Athos.</p>

<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karyes_(Athos)" title="Karyes (Athos)">Karyes</a></li>
